<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Atmospheric methane</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="650" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png/300px-Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png" concentrations up to May 2018: A monthly peak of 1887.65 ppb was reached in November 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Compilation of <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">paleo-climatology</a> data of methane</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_distribution_of_atmospheric_methane.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="496" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Global_distribution_of_atmospheric_methane.jpg/600px-Global_distribution_of_atmospheric_methane.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Methane observations from 2005 to 2014 showing the seasonal variations and the difference between northern and southern hemispheres</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AtmosphericMethane.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="633" data-file-width="540" height="352" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/AtmosphericMethane.png/300px-AtmosphericMethane.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/AtmosphericMethane.png/450px-AtmosphericMethane.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/AtmosphericMethane.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Computer models showing the amount of methane (parts per million by volume) at the surface (top) and in the stratosphere (bottom)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Atmospheric methane</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> present in earth's <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Atmospheric methane concentrations are of interest because it is one of the most potent <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> in earth's atmosphere. The 100-year <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> of methane is 28.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gunnar2013_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gunnar2013-4">[4]</a></sup> That is, over a 100-year period, it traps 28 times more heat per mass unit than <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and 32 times the effect when accounting for <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosol</a> interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Global methane concentrations had risen from 722 parts per billion (ppb) in pre-industrial times to 1800 ppb by 2011, an increase by a factor of 2.5 and the highest value in at least 800,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Its concentration is higher in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> since most sources (both natural and human) are located on land and the Northern Hemisphere has more land mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Volodin2015_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Volodin2015-7">[7]</a></sup> The concentrations vary seasonally, with, for example, a minimum in the northern tropics during April−May mainly due to removal by the <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">hydroxyl radical</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crevoisier2012_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crevoisier2012-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_history" title="Earth's history">earth's history</a> carbon dioxide and methane likely produced a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>. The carbon dioxide would have been produced by volcanoes and the methane by early microbes. During this time, earth's earliest life appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gale_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-9">[9]</a></sup> These first, ancient bacteria added to the methane concentration by converting hydrogen and carbon dioxide into methane and water. Oxygen did not become a major part of the atmosphere until photosynthetic organisms evolved later in earth's history. With no oxygen, methane stayed in the atmosphere longer and at higher concentrations than it does today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pavlov2003_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlov2003-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methane forms near the Earth's surface, and it is carried into the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a> by rising air. Uncontrolled build-up of methane in earth's atmosphere is naturally checked—although human influence can upset this natural regulation—by methane's reaction with hydroxyl radicals formed from <a href="/wiki/Singlet_oxygen" title="Singlet oxygen">singlet oxygen</a> atoms and with water vapor.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane_as_a_greenhouse_gas">Methane as a greenhouse gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Methane as a greenhouse gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>. In particular: Needs rewrite based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fifth_Assessment_Report" title="Fifth Assessment Report">IPCC AR5</a>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(September 2018)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Methane in the earth's atmosphere is a strong <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> (GWP) 104 times greater than CO<sub>2</sub> in a 20-year time frame; methane is not as persistent a gas as CO<sub>2</sub> and tails off to about GWP of 28 for a 100-year time frame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gunnar2013_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gunnar2013-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atul2000_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atul2000-11">[11]</a></sup> This means that a methane emission will have 28 times the impact on temperature of a carbon dioxide emission of the same mass over the following 100 years. Methane has a large effect but for a relatively brief period, having an estimated lifetime of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008280862640000000♠</span>8.9<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.6 years</span> in the atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wuebbles_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wuebbles-12">[12]</a></sup> whereas carbon dioxide has a small effect for a long period, having an estimated lifetime of over 100 years.
</p><p>The concentration of methane in earth's atmosphere has increased by about 150 percent since 1750, and it now accounts for 20% of the total <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> from all of the long-lived and globally mixed greenhouse gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical_summary_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Technical_summary-13">[13]</a></sup> According to NOAA the atmospheric methane concentration was measured at 1890 ppb in Ireland (Northern Hemisphere) and 1760 ppb (Tasmania, Southern Hemisphere) in 2013 – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_methane_release" title="Arctic methane release">arctic methane release</a> from melting <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">methane clathrates</a> has played a role, but other recent emissions include peat fires in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asian_haze" title="Southeast Asian haze">Southeast Asia</a>. In all of the 420,000 year time-period before the industrial era, the atmospheric methane concentration has been less than half the current level,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> including the period since the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chappellaz1993_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chappellaz1993-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_methane_cycle">Global methane cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Global methane cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:902px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GMCdiagram.jpg"><img alt="Global Methane Cycle. A diagram of the Global Methane Cycle." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="687" data-file-width="1506" height="411" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/GMCdiagram.jpg/900px-GMCdiagram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/GMCdiagram.jpg/1350px-GMCdiagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/GMCdiagram.jpg 2x" width="900"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GMCdiagram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This simple diagram depicts the flow of methane from sources into the atmosphere as well as the sinks that consume methane. More detailed explanations of each source and sink are covered in later sections.</div></div></div></div>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">Permafrost</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciers" title="Glaciers">glaciers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WalterAnthony2012_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-WalterAnthony2012-17">[17]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_cores" title="Ice cores">ice cores</a> – A source that slowly releases methane trapped in frozen environments as global temperatures rise.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wetlands" title="Wetlands">Wetlands</a> – Warm temperatures and moist environments are ideal for methane production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_fire" title="Forest fire">Forest fire</a> – Mass burning of organic matter releases methane into the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_paddies" title="Rice paddies">Rice paddies</a> – The warmer and moister the rice field, the more methane is produced.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animals" title="Animals">Animals</a> – Microorganisms breaking down difficult to digest material in the guts of ruminant livestock and termites produce methane that is then released during defecation.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">Plants</a> – While methane can be consumed in soil before being released into the atmosphere, plants allow for direct travel of methane up through the roots and leaves and into the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Plants may also be direct producers of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landfills" title="Landfills">Landfills</a> – Decaying organic matter and anaerobic conditions cause landfills to be a significant source of methane.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">Waste water treatment facilities</a> – Anaerobic treatment of organic compounds in the water results in the production of methane.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">Hydroxyl radical</a> – OH in the atmosphere is the largest sink for atmospheric methane as well as one of the most significant sources of water vapor in the upper atmosphere.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">Chlorine</a> <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radical</a> – Free chlorine in the atmosphere also reacts with methane.</li></ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emissions_accounting_of_methane">Emissions accounting of methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Emissions accounting of methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The balance between sources and sinks of methane is not yet fully understood. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPCC_Working_Group_I" title="IPCC Working Group I">IPCC Working Group I</a> stated in chapter 2 of the Fourth Assessment Report that there are "large uncertainties in the current bottom-up estimates of components of the global source", and the balance between sources and sinks is not yet well known. The most important sink in the methane cycle is reaction with the hydroxyl radical, which is produced photochemically in the atmosphere. Production of this radical is not fully understood and has a large effect on atmospheric concentrations. This uncertainty is exemplified by observations that have shown between the year 2000 and 2006 increases in atmospheric concentration of methane ceased, for reasons still being investigated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirschke2013_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirschke2013-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Various sources give the following values for <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane emissions</a>:
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Methane_Sources.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="532" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Methane_Sources.jpg/400px-Methane_Sources.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/Methane_Sources.jpg 1.5x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Methane_Sources.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Natural and anthropogenic methane sources, according to the NASA Goddard Institute for Space Studies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Methane_sources_graph_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Methane_sources_graph-29">[25]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_sources_of_atmospheric_methane">Natural sources of atmospheric methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Natural sources of atmospheric methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Any process that results in the production of methane and its release into the atmosphere can be considered a "source." The two main processes that are responsible for methane production occur as a result of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microorganisms" title="Microorganisms">microorganisms</a> <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion" title="Anaerobic digestion">anaerobically</a> converting organic compounds into methane.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Methanogenesis">Methanogenesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Methanogenesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most ecological emissions of methane relate directly to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methanogens" title="Methanogens">methanogens</a> generating methane in warm, moist soils as well as in the digestive tracts of certain animals.
Methanogens are methane producing microorganisms. In order to produce energy, they use an anaerobic process called methanogenesis. This process is used in lieu of aerobic, or with oxygen, processes because methanogens are unable to metabolise in the presence of even small concentrations of oxygen. When acetate is broken down in methanogenesis, the result is the release of methane into the surrounding environment.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Methanogenesis" title="Methanogenesis">Methanogenesis</a></i>, the scientific term for methane production, occurs primarily in anaerobic conditions because of the lack of availability of other oxidants. In these conditions, <a href="/wiki/Microscopic_scale" title="Microscopic scale">microscopic</a> organisms called <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> use acetate and hydrogen to break down essential resources<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (February 2016)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup> in a process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a>.
</p><p><i>Acetoclastic methanogenesis</i> – certain archaea cleave <a href="/wiki/Acetate" title="Acetate">acetate</a> produced during anaerobic fermentation to yield methane and carbon dioxide.
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>3</sub>C-COOH → CH<sub>4</sub> + CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p><i>Hydrogenotrophic methanogenesis</i> – archaea <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidize" title="Oxidize">oxidize</a> hydrogen with carbon dioxide to yield methane and water.
</p>
<dl><dd>4H<sub>2</sub> + CO<sub>2</sub> → CH<sub>4</sub> + 2H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>While acetoclastic methanogenesis and hydrogenotrophic methanogenesis are the two major source reactions for atmospheric methane, other minor biological methane source reactions also occur. For example, it has been discovered that <a href="/wiki/Epicuticular_wax" title="Epicuticular wax">leaf surface wax</a> exposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_radiation" title="UV radiation">UV radiation</a> in the presence of oxygen is an aerobic source of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wetlands">Wetlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Wetlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wetland_Methane_Emissions" title="Wetland Methane Emissions">Wetland Methane Emissions</a></div>
<p>Wetlands account for approximately 20 percent of atmospheric methane through emissions from soils and plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bubier_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bubier-31">[27]</a></sup> Wetlands counteract the sinking action that normally occurs with soil because of the high water table. The level of the water table represents the boundary between anaerobic methane production and aerobic methane consumption. When the water table is low, the methane generated within the wetland soil has to come up through the soil and get past a deeper layer of methanotrophic bacteria, thereby reducing emission. Methane transport by vascular plants can bypass this aerobic layer, thus increasing emission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macdonald1998_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macdonald1998-32">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gedney2004_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gedney2004-33">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ruminant animals, particularly cows and sheep, contain bacteria in their gastrointestinal systems that help to break down plant material. Some of these microorganisms use the acetate from the plant material to produce methane, and because these bacteria live in the stomachs and intestines of ruminants, whenever the animal "burps" or defecates, it emits methane as well. Based upon a study in the <a href="/wiki/Snowy_Mountains" title="Snowy Mountains">Snowy Mountains</a> region, the amount of methane emitted by one cow is equivalent to the amount of methane that around 3.4 hectares of <a href="#Removal_processes">methanotrophic bacteria</a> can consume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MasonJones2012_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-MasonJones2012-34">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">Termites</a> also contain methanogenic microorganisms in their gut. However, some of these microorganisms are so unique that they live nowhere else in the world except in the third gut of termites. These microorganisms also break down biotic components to produce <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, as well as methane byproduct. However, unlike ruminants who lose 20 percent of the energy from the plants they eat, termites only lose 2 percent of their energy in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gut_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gut-35">[31]</a></sup> Thus comparatively, termites do not have to eat as much food as ruminants to obtain the same amount of energy, and give off proportionally less methane.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">Plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Living plants (e.g. forests) have recently been identified as a potentially important source of methane, possibly being responsible for approximately 10 to 30 percent of atmospheric methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPPC2_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPPC2-36">[32]</a></sup> A 2006 paper calculated emissions of 62–236 Tg <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum" title="Annum">a</a><sup>−1</sup>, and "this newly identified source may have important implications".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup> However the authors stress <i>"our findings are preliminary with regard to the methane emission strength"</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>These findings have been called into question in a 2007 paper which found <i>"there is no evidence for substantial aerobic methane emission by terrestrial plants, maximally 0.3% of the previously published values"</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the details of plant methane emissions have yet to be confirmed, plants as a significant methane source would help fill in the gaps of previous global methane budgets as well as explain large plumes of methane that have been observed over the tropics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPPC2_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPPC2-36">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-41">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In wetlands, where the rate of methane production is high, plants help methane travel into the atmosphere—acting like inverted lightning rods as they direct the gas up through the soil and into the air. They are also suspected to produce methane themselves, but because the plants would have to use aerobic conditions to produce methane, the process itself is still unidentified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmichael2014_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carmichael2014-42">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane_gas_from_methane_clathrates">Methane gas from methane clathrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Methane gas from methane clathrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_methane_release" title="Arctic methane release">Arctic methane release</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clathrate_gun_effect" title="Clathrate gun effect">Clathrate gun effect</a></div>
<p>At high pressures, such as are found on the bottom of the ocean, methane forms a solid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clathrate" title="Clathrate">clathrate</a> with water, known as <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">methane hydrate</a>. An unknown, but possibly very large quantity of methane is trapped in this form in ocean sediments. The release of large volumes of methane gas from such sediments into the atmosphere has been suggested as a possible cause for rapid <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> events in the Earth's distant past, such as the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum" title="Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum">Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum</a> of 55 million years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen2014_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen2014-43">[39]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Dying" title="Great Dying">Great Dying</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benton2003_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benton2003-44">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Theories suggest that should global warming cause them to heat up sufficiently, all of this methane gas could again be released into the atmosphere. Since methane gas is twenty-five times stronger (for a given weight, averaged over 100 years) than <span class="chemf nowrap">CO <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> as a greenhouse gas; this would immensely magnify the greenhouse effect. However, most of this reservoir of hydrates appears isolated from changes to the surface climate, so any such release is likely to happen over geological timescales of a millennium or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archer2007_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Archer2007-45">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Permafrost">Permafrost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Permafrost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CH4.BRW.Monthly.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="650" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/CH4.BRW.Monthly.png/300px-CH4.BRW.Monthly.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/CH4.BRW.Monthly.png/450px-CH4.BRW.Monthly.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/CH4.BRW.Monthly.png/600px-CH4.BRW.Monthly.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CH4.BRW.Monthly.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arctic methane concentrations up to May 2018</div></div></div>
<p>Methane that gets frozen in <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> – land that is frozen for several years at a time – is slowly released from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bogs" title="Bogs">bogs</a> as the permafrost melts. With rising global temperatures, the amount of permafrost melting and releasing methane continues to increase.
</p><p>Although records of permafrost are limited, recent years (1999 to 2007) have seen record thawing of permafrost in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>. Measurements during 2006 in Siberia show that the methane released is five times greater than previously estimated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[42]</a></sup> Melting <a href="/wiki/Yedoma" title="Yedoma">yedoma</a>, a type of permafrost, is a significant source of atmospheric methane (about 4 Tg of CH<sub>4</sub> per year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Woods_Hole_Research_Center" title="Woods Hole Research Center">Woods Hole Research Center</a>, citing two 2015 studies on permafrost carbon says there may be a self-reinforcing tipping point where an estimated equivalent of 205 gigatons of carbon dioxide in the form of methane could cause up to 0.5 °C (up to 0.9 °F) warming by the end of the century, which would trigger more warming. Permafrost contains almost twice as much carbon as is present in the atmosphere. The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> does not adequately account for arctic methane in permafrost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abraham_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abraham-48">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropogenic_sources_of_atmospheric_methane">Anthropogenic sources of atmospheric methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Anthropogenic sources of atmospheric methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Slightly over half of the total emission is due to human activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical_summary_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Technical_summary-13">[13]</a></sup> Since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> humans have had a major impact on concentrations of atmospheric methane, increasing atmospheric concentrations roughly 250%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell2013_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell2013-49">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_conversion">Ecological conversion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ecological conversion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Conversion of forests and natural environments into agricultural plots increases the amount of nitrogen in the soil, which inhibits <a href="#Removal_processes">methane oxidation</a>, weakening the ability of the methanotrophic bacteria in the soil to act as sinks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loic2013_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loic2013-50">[46]</a></sup> Additionally, by changing the level of the water table, humans can directly affect the soil’s ability to act as a source or sink. The relationship between water table levels and methane emission is explained in the wetlands section of natural sources.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Farm_animals">Farm animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Farm animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A 2006 UN FAO report reported that livestock generate more greenhouse gases as measured in CO<sub>2</sub> equivalents than the entire transportation sector. Livestock accounts for 9 percent of anthropogenic CO<sub>2</sub>, 65 percent of anthropogenic nitrous oxide and 37 percent of anthropogenic methane. A senior UN official and co-author of the report, Henning Steinfeld, said "Livestock are one of the most significant contributors to today's most serious environmental problems."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent NASA research has confirmed the vital role of <a href="/wiki/Enteric_fermentation" title="Enteric fermentation">enteric fermentation</a> in livestock on global warming. "We understand that other greenhouse gases apart from carbon dioxide are important for climate change today," said <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Schmidt" title="Gavin Schmidt">Gavin Schmidt</a>, the lead author of the study and a researcher at NASA's Goddard Institute for Space Studies in New York, NY and Columbia University's Center for Climate Systems Research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup> Other recent peer reviewed NASA research published in the journal <i>Science</i> has also indicated that the contribution of methane to global warming has been underestimated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nicholas Stern, the author of the 2006 Stern Review on climate change has stated "people will need to turn vegetarian if the world is to conquer climate change".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup> President of the National Academy of Sciences <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Cicerone" title="Ralph Cicerone">Ralph Cicerone</a> (an atmospheric scientist), has indicated the contribution of methane by livestock <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eructation" title="Eructation">eructation</a> to global warming is a "serious topic." Cicerone states "Methane is the second-most-important greenhouse gas in the atmosphere now. The population of beef cattle and dairy cattle has grown so much that methane from cows now is big. This is not a trivial issue."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Approximately 5% of the methane is released via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flatus" title="Flatus">flatus</a>, whereas the other 95% is released via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eructation" title="Eructation">eructation</a>. Vaccines are under development to reduce the amount introduced through eructation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rice_agriculture">Rice agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Rice agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Due to a continuously growing world population, rice agriculture has become one of the most significant anthropogenic sources of methane. With warm weather and water-logged soil, rice paddies act like wetlands, but are generated by humans for the purpose of food production. Due to the swamp-like environment of rice fields, these paddies yield 50-100 million metric tons of methane emission each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rice_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rice-58">[54]</a></sup> This means that rice agriculture is responsible for approximately 15 to 20 percent of anthropogenic methane emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rice_Agriculture_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rice_Agriculture-59">[55]</a></sup> An article written by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_F._Ruddiman" title="William F. Ruddiman">William F. Ruddiman</a> explores the possibility that methane emissions began to rise as a result of anthropogenic activity 5000 years ago when ancient cultures started to settle and use agriculture, rice irrigation in particular, as a primary food source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruddiman_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruddiman-60">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Landfills">Landfills</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Landfills">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Due to the large collections of organic matter and availability of anaerobic conditions, landfills are the third largest source of atmospheric methane in the United States, accounting for roughly 18.2% of methane emissions globally in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup> When waste is first added to a landfill, oxygen is abundant and thus undergoes aerobic decomposition; during which time very little methane is produced. However, generally within a year oxygen levels are depleted and anaerobic conditions dominate the landfill allowing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methanogens" title="Methanogens">methanogens</a> to takeover the decomposition process. These methanogens emit methane into the atmosphere and even after the landfill is closed, the mass amount of decaying matter allows the methanogens to continue producing methane for years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_water_treatment">Waste water treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Waste water treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Waste water treatment facilities act to remove organic matter, solids, pathogens, and chemical hazards as a result of human contamination. Methane emission in waste treatment facilities occurs as a result of anaerobic treatments of organic compounds and anaerobic <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradation</a> of sludge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandE_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-SandE-63">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biomass_burning">Biomass burning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Biomass burning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Incomplete burning of both living and dead organic matter results in the emission of methane. While natural wildfires can contribute to methane emissions, the bulk majority of biomass burning occurs as a result of humans – including everything from accidental burnings by civilians to deliberate burnings used to clear out land to biomass burnings occurring as a result of destroying waste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-41">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_gas_distribution">Natural gas distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Natural gas distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Methane is a primary component of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, and thus during the production, processing, storage, transmission, and distribution of natural gas, a significant amount of methane is lost into the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandE_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-SandE-63">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the EPA Inventory of U.S Greenhouse Gases and Sinks: 1990-2009 report, dated April 2011, methane emissions from oil and natural gas systems make up 3.8 percent of total greenhouse gas emissions in the United States. Methane emissions occur in all sectors of the natural gas industry, from drilling and production, through gathering and processing and transmission, to distribution. These emissions occur through normal operation, routine maintenance, fugitive leaks, system upsets, and venting of equipment. In the oil industry, some underground crude contains natural gas that is entrained in the oil at high reservoir pressures. When oil is removed from the reservoir, associated natural gas is produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA0412_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA0412-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQSTAR_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAQSTAR-65">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, the inventory shows that the oil and natural gas industry emitted 624 billion cubic feet (Bcf) of methane in 2009. Of this amount, 63% was from production operations, 7% from processing, 18% from transmission and storage systems, and 12% from distribution systems. Oil and natural gas systems are the largest human-made source of methane emissions (37 percent) in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA0412_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-EPA0412-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQSTAR_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAQSTAR-65">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, according to the EPA Global Anthropogenic Non CO<sub>2</sub> Greenhouse Gas Emissions: 1990-2020 report, dated June 2006, oil and natural gas operations are a significant source of global methane emissions, constituting approximately 18 percent of total human-made methane emissions. The report also indicates that in 2005, global oil and natural gas methane emissions totaled approximately 82 billion cubic meters (Bcm), or 2,896 billion cubic feet (Bcf), equivalent to about 1,165 million tonnes carbon dioxide equivalent (MtCO2e).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQSTAR_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-FAQSTAR-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA430R06003_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA430R06003-66">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent estimates of natural gas losses during distribution are as high as 9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gas flares are a tool the energy industry can use to <a href="/wiki/Gas_flare#Environmental_benefit" title="Gas flare">reduce its environmental impact.</a>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane_slip_from_gas_engines">Methane slip from gas engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Methane slip from gas engines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The use of natural gas and biogas in ICE (<a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">Internal combustion engine</a>) for such applications as electricity production / cogeneration / CHP (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combined_Heat_and_Power" title="Combined Heat and Power">Combined Heat and Power</a>) and heavy vehicles or marine vessels such as <a href="/wiki/LNG_carrier" title="LNG carrier">LNG carrier</a>s using the boil off gas for propulsion, emits a certain percentage of UHC, <a href="/wiki/Unburned_hydrocarbon" title="Unburned hydrocarbon">unburned hydrocarbon</a> of which 85% is methane. The climate issues of using gas to fuel ICE may offset or even cancel out the advantages of less CO<sub>2</sub> and particle emissions is described in this <a class="external text" href="http://ec.europa.eu/environment/integration/research/newsalert/pdf/methane_emissions_from_lng_powered_ships_higher_than_current_marine_fuel_oils_444na4_en.pdf" rel="nofollow">2016 EU Issue Paper on methane slip from marine engines</a>: "Emissions of unburnt methane (known as the ‘methane slip’) were around 7g per kg LNG at higher engine loads, rising to 23–36g at lower loads. This increase could be due to slow combustion at lower temperatures, which allows small quantities of gas to avoid the combustion process". Road vehicles run more on low load than marine engines causing relatively higher methane slip.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_mining">Coal mining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Coal mining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2014 <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> researchers reported the discovery of a 2,500 square miles (6,500 km<sup>2</sup>) <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> cloud floating over the Four Corners region of the south-west United States. The discovery was based on data from the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>’s Scanning Imaging Absorption Spectrometer for Atmospheric Chartography instrument from 2002 to 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-68">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The report concluded that "the source is likely from established gas, coal, and <a href="/wiki/Coalbed_methane" title="Coalbed methane">coalbed methane</a> mining and processing." The region emitted 590,000 metric tons of methane every year between 2002 and 2012—almost 3.5 times the widely used estimates in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>’s Emissions Database for Global Atmospheric Research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-68">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Removal_processes">Removal processes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Removal processes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Any process that consumes methane from the atmosphere can be considered a "sink" of atmospheric methane. The most prominent of these processes occur as a result of methane either being destroyed in the atmosphere or broken down in soil. Humans have yet to act as any significant sink of atmospheric methane.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MethaneSinkPieChart.jpg"><img alt="A colored pie chart with 4 distinct sections representing the major sinks of atmospheric methane." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="192" data-file-width="233" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/MethaneSinkPieChart.jpg/220px-MethaneSinkPieChart.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/MethaneSinkPieChart.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MethaneSinkPieChart.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pie chart demonstrating the relative effects of various sinks of atmospheric methane</div></div></div>
<p><i>Reaction with the hydroxyl radical</i> – The major removal mechanism of methane from the atmosphere involves <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radical chemistry</a>; it reacts with the <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">hydroxyl radical</a> (<b>·</b>OH) in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> or <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a> to create the <a href="/wiki/Methyl_group" title="Methyl group"><b>·</b>CH<sub>3</sub></a> radical and water vapor. In addition to being the largest known sink for atmospheric methane, this reaction is one of the most important sources of water vapor in the upper atmosphere.
</p>
<div style="text-align: center;"> <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span> + <b>·</b>OH → <b>·</b>CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span> + H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> </div>
<p>This reaction in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> gives a methane lifetime of 9.6 years. Two more minor sinks are soil sinks (160 year lifetime) and stratospheric loss by reaction with <b>·</b>OH, <b>·</b>Cl and <b>·</b>O<sup>1</sup>D in the stratosphere (120 year lifetime), giving a net lifetime of 8.4 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trace_Gases_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trace_Gases-23">[23]</a></sup> Oxidation of methane is the main source of water vapor in the upper stratosphere (beginning at pressure levels around 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KPa" title="KPa">kPa</a>).
</p><p>The methyl radical formed in the above reaction will, during normal daytime conditions in the troposphere, usually react with another hydroxyl radical to form <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>. Note that this is not strictly oxidative <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> as described previously. Formaldehyde can react again with a hydroxyl radical to form carbon dioxide and more water vapor. Sidechains in these reactions may interact with <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> compounds that will likely produce <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>, thus supplanting radicals required in the initial reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_sinks_of_atmospheric_methane">Natural sinks of atmospheric methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Natural sinks of atmospheric methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most natural sinks occur as a result of chemical reactions in the atmosphere as well as oxidation by methane consuming bacteria in Earth’s soils.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Methanotrophs_in_soils">Methanotrophs in soils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Methanotrophs in soils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Soils act as a major sink for atmospheric methane through the methanotrophic bacteria that reside within them. This occurs with two different types of bacteria. "High capacity-low affinity" methanotrophic bacteria grow in areas of high methane concentration, such as waterlogged soils in wetlands and other moist environments. And in areas of low methane concentration, "low capacity-high affinity" methanotrophic bacteria make use of the methane in the atmosphere to grow, rather than relying on methane in their immediate environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoilSink_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-SoilSink-70">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Forest soils act as good sinks for atmospheric methane because soils are optimally moist for methanotroph activity, and the movement of gases between soil and atmosphere (soil diffusivity) is high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoilSink_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-SoilSink-70">[66]</a></sup> With a lower water table, any methane in the soil has to make it past the methanotrophic bacteria before it can reach the atmosphere.
</p><p>Wetland soils, however, are often sources of atmospheric methane rather than sinks because the water table is much higher, and the methane can be diffused fairly easily into the air without having to compete with the soil’s methanotrophs.
</p><p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methanotrophic" title="Methanotrophic">Methanotrophic</a> bacteria in soils</i> – Methanotrophic bacteria that reside within soil use methane as a source of carbon in methane oxidation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoilSink_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-SoilSink-70">[66]</a></sup> Methane oxidation allows methanotrophic bacteria to use methane as a source of energy, reacting methane with oxygen and as a result producing carbon dioxide and water.
</p>
<dl><dd>CH<sub>4</sub> + 2O<sub>2</sub> → CO<sub>2</sub> + 2H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Troposphere">Troposphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Troposphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The most effective sink of atmospheric methane is the hydroxyl radical in the troposphere, or the lowest portion of Earth’s atmosphere. As methane rises into the air, it reacts with the hydroxyl radical to create water vapor and carbon dioxide. The lifespan of methane in the atmosphere was estimated at 9.6 years as of 2001; however, increasing emissions of methane over time reduce the concentration of the hydroxyl radical in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-41">[37]</a></sup> With less OH˚ to react with, the lifespan of methane could also increase, resulting in greater concentrations of atmospheric methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes2013_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes2013-71">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stratosphere">Stratosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Stratosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>If it is not destroyed in the troposphere, methane will last approximately 120 years before it is eventually destroyed in Earth’s next atmospheric layer: the stratosphere. Destruction in the stratosphere occurs the same way that it does in the troposphere: methane is oxidized to produce carbon dioxide and water vapor. Based on balloon-borne measurements since 1978, the abundance of stratospheric methane has increased by <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999134000000000000♠</span>13.4%<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>3.6%</span> between 1978 and 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rohs2006_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rohs2006-72">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reaction_with_free_chlorine">Reaction with free chlorine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Reaction with free chlorine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Methane also reacts with natural chlorine gas in the atmosphere to produce <a href="/wiki/Chloromethane" title="Chloromethane">chloromethane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> (HCl). This process is known as free radical halogenations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-73">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>CH<sub>4</sub> + Cl<sub>2</sub> → CH<sub>3</sub>Cl + HCl</dd></dl>
<p>The HCl produced in this reaction leads to catalytic <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> destruction in the stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rohs2006_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rohs2006-72">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Patterns_of_methane_change_over_time">Patterns of methane change over time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Patterns of methane change over time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since the 1800s, atmospheric methane concentrations have increased annually at a rate of about 0.9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bubier_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bubier-31">[27]</a></sup> Long term atmospheric measurements of methane by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NOAA" title="NOAA">NOAA</a> show that the build up of methane has slowed dramatically over the decade leading to 2006, after nearly tripling since pre-industrial times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup> Although scientists have yet to pinpoint the exact reason(s) for this sudden drop in growth rates, it is thought that this reduction is due to reduced industrial emissions and drought in wetland areas.
</p><p>The only exceptions to this drop in growth rate occurred in 1991 and 1998 when growth rates increased suddenly to 14-15 nmol/mol per year for those years, nearly double the growth rates of the years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPPC2_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-IPPC2-36">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1991 spike is believed to have occurred due to the volcanic eruption of Mt. Pinatubo in June of that year. Volcanoes affect atmospheric methane emissions when they erupt, releasing ash and sulfur dioxide into the air. As a result, photochemistry of plants is affected and the removal of methane via the tropospheric hydroxyl radical is reduced. However, growth rates quickly fell due to lower temperatures and global reduction in rainfall.
</p><p>The cause of the 1998 spike is unresolved, but scientists are currently attributing it to a combination of increased wetland and rice field emissions as well as an increased amount of biomass burning. 1998 was also the warmest year since surface temperatures were first recorded, suggesting that anomalously high temperatures can induce elevated methane emission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC7_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC7-75">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Data from 2007 suggested methane concentrations were beginning to rise again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup> This was confirmed in 2010 when a study showed methane levels were on the rise for the 3 years 2007 to 2009. After a decade of near-zero growth in methane levels, "globally averaged atmospheric methane increased by [approximately] 7 nmol/mol per year during 2007 and 2008. During the first half of 2009, globally averaged atmospheric CH<sub>4</sub> was [approximately] 7 nmol/mol greater than it was in 2008, suggesting that the increase will continue in 2009."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methane emissions levels vary greatly depending on the local geography. For both natural and anthropogenic sources, higher temperatures and higher water levels result in the anaerobic environment that is necessary for methane production.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_methane_cycles">Natural methane cycles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Natural methane cycles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Emissions of methane into the atmosphere are directly related to temperature and moisture. Thus, the natural environmental changes that occur during seasonal change act as a major control of methane emission. Additionally, even changes in temperature during the day can affect the amount of methane that is produced and consumed.
</p><p>For example, plants that produce methane can emit as much as two to four times more methane during the day than during the night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bubier_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bubier-31">[27]</a></sup> This is directly related to the fact that plants tend to rely on solar energy to enact chemical processes.
</p><p>Additionally, methane emissions are affected by the level of water sources. Seasonal flooding during the spring and summer naturally increases the amount of methane released into the air.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_anthropogenic_sources">Changes in anthropogenic sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Changes in anthropogenic sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most clearly identified rise in atmospheric methane as a result of human activity occurred in the 1700s during the industrial revolution. As technology increased at a considerable rate, humans began to build factories and plants, burn fossil fuels for energy, and clear out forests and other vegetation for the purpose of building and agriculture. This growth continued to rise at a rate of almost 1 percent per year until around 1990 when growth rates dropped to almost zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPPC2_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-IPPC2-36">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2003 article from William F. Ruddiman, however, indicates that the anthropogenic change in methane may have started 5000 years prior to the industrial revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruddiman_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruddiman-60">[56]</a></sup> The methane <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">insolation</a> cycles of the ice core remained stable and predictable until 5000 years ago, most likely due to some anthropogenic effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruddiman_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ruddiman-60">[56]</a></sup> Ruddiman suggests that the transition of humans from hunter gatherers into agricultural farming was the first instance of humans affecting methane concentration in the atmosphere. Ruddiman’s hypothesis is supported by the fact that early rice irrigation occurred approximately 5000 years ago—the same time the ice core cycles lost their predictability. Due to the inefficiency of humans first learning how to grow rice, extensive rice paddies would have been needed to feed even a small population. These, over-flooded and filled with weeds, would have resulted in huge methane emitting wetlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruddiman_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ruddiman-60">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another source of methane emissions has been identified in Russia. Near Yamburg and Urengoy exist gas fields with a methane concentration of 97 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wupp_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wupp-78">[74]</a></sup> The gas obtained from these fields is taken and exported to Western and Central Europe through an extensive pipeline system known as the Trans-Siberian natural gas pipeline system. In accordance with the IPCC and other natural gas emissions control groups, measurements had to be taken throughout the pipeline to measure methane emissions from technological discharges and leaks at the pipeline fittings and vents. Although the majority of the natural gas leaks were carbon dioxide, a significant amount of methane was also being consistently released from the pipeline as a result of leaks and breakdowns. In 2001, natural gas emissions from the pipeline and natural gas transportation system accounted for 1 percent of the natural gas produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wupp_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wupp-78">[74]</a></sup> Fortunately, between 2001 and 2005, this number reduced to 0.7 percent, and even the 2001 value is still significantly less than that of 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wupp_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wupp-78">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>
However that may be, transportation is only part of the problem. Howarth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[75]</a></sup> <i>et al.</i> have argued that: </p><blockquote><p> We believe the preponderance of evidence indicates shale gas has a larger GHG [green house gas] footprint than conventional gas, considered over any time scale. The GHG footprint of shale gas also exceeds that of oil or coal when considered at decadal time scales, […] </p></blockquote><p> For subsequent works confirming these results see Howarth's <i>"A bridge to nowhere: methane emissions and the greenhouse gas footprint of natural gas,"</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[76]</a></sup> and "<i>Methane emissions and climatic warming risk from hydraulic fracturing and shale gas development: implications for policy.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[77]</a></sup>
A 2013 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[78]</a></sup> by Miller <i>et al.</i> indicates that current greenhouse gas reduction policies in the US are based on what appear to be significant underestimates of anthropogenic methane emissions. The authors state: </p><blockquote><p> We find greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture and fossil fuel extraction and processing (<i>i.e.,</i> oil and/or natural gas) are likely a factor of two or greater than cited in existing studies. </p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impacts">Impacts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Impacts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The direct radiative greenhouse gas forcing effect has been estimated at 0.5 W/m<sup>2</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AR4_Fig_2.4_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-AR4_Fig_2.4-83">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methane is a strong GHG with a global warming potential 84 times greater than CO<sub>2</sub> in a 20-year time frame; Methane is not as persistent a gas and tails off to about 28 for a 100-year time frame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gunnar2013_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gunnar2013-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the direct heating effect and the normal feedbacks, the methane breaks down to carbon dioxide and water. This water is often above the tropopause where little water usually reaches. Ramanathan (1988)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramanathan_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramanathan-84">[80]</a></sup> notes that both water and ice clouds, when formed at cold lower stratospheric temperatures, are extremely efficient in enhancing the atmospheric greenhouse effect. He also notes that there is a distinct possibility that large increases in future methane may lead to a surface warming that increases nonlinearly with the methane concentration.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ozone_layer">Ozone layer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Ozone layer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Methane also affects the degradation of the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>, when methane is transformed into water in the stratosphere. This process is enhanced by global warming, because warmer air holds more water vapor than colder air, so the amount of water vapor in the atmosphere increases as it is warmed by the greenhouse effect. Climate models also indicate that greenhouse gases such as carbon dioxide and methane may enhance the transport of water into the stratosphere; though this is not fully understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane_management_techniques">Methane management techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Methane management techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In an effort to mitigate climate change, humans have started to develop alternative methods and medicines.
</p><p>For example, in order to counteract the amount of methane that ruminants give off, a type of drug called monensin (marketed as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rumensin" title="Rumensin">rumensin</a>™) has been developed. This drug is classified as an ionophore, which is an antibiotic that is naturally produced by a harmless bacteria strain. This drug not only improves feed efficiency but also reduces the amount of methane gas emitted from the animal and its manure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rumensin_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rumensin-86">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to medicine, specific manure management techniques have been developed to counteract emissions from livestock manure. Educational resources have begun to be provided for small farms. Management techniques include daily pickup and storage of manure in a completely closed off storage facility that will prevent runoff from making it into bodies of water. The manure can then be kept in storage until it is either reused for fertilizer or taken away and stored in an offsite compost. Nutrient levels of various animal manures are provided for optimal use as compost for gardens and agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bradley-87">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In order to reduce effects on methane oxidation in soil, several steps can be taken. Controlling the usage of nitrogen enhancing fertilizer and reducing the amount of nitrogen pollution into the air can both lower inhibition of methane oxidation. Additionally, using drier growing conditions for crops such as rice and selecting strains of crops that produce more food per unit area can reduce the amount of land with ideal conditions for methanogenesis. Careful selection of areas of land conversion (for example, plowing down forests to create agricultural fields) can also reduce the destruction of major areas of methane oxidation.
</p><p>To counteract methane emissions from landfills, on March 12, 1996, the EPA (Environmental Protection Agency) added the "Landfill Rule" to the Clean Air Act. This rule requires large landfills that have ever accepted <a href="/wiki/Municipal_solid_waste" title="Municipal solid waste">municipal solid waste</a>, have been used as of November 8, 1987, can hold at least 2.5 million metric tons of waste with a volume greater than 2.5 million cubic meters, and/or have nonmethane organic compound (NMOC) emissions of at least 50 metric tons per year to collect and combust emitted <a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">landfill gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landfill_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landfill-88">[84]</a></sup> This set of requirements excludes 96% of the landfills in the USA. While the direct result of this is landfills reducing emission of non-methane compounds that form smog, the indirect result is reduction of methane emissions as well.
</p><p>Furthermore, in an attempt to absorb the methane that is already being produced from landfills, experiments in which nutrients were added to the soil to allow <a href="/wiki/Methanotroph" title="Methanotroph">methanotrophs</a> to thrive have been conducted. These nutrient supplemented landfills have been shown to act as a small scale methane sink, allowing the abundance of methanotrophs to sponge the methane from the air to use as energy, effectively reducing the landfill's emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>To reduce emissions from the natural gas industries, the EPA developed the Natural Gas STAR Program, also known as Gas STAR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandE_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-SandE-63">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another program was also developed by the EPA to reduce emissions from coal mining. The Coalbed Methane Outreach Program (CMOP) helps and encourages the mining industry to find ways to use or sell <a href="/wiki/Coalbed_methane" title="Coalbed methane">methane</a> that would otherwise be released from the coal mine into the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandE_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-SandE-63">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>A portable methane detector has been developed which, mounted in a vehicle, can detect excess levels of methane in the ambient atmosphere and differentiate between natural methane from rotting vegetation or manure and gas leaks. As of 2013 the technology was being deployed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Gas_%26_Electric" title="Pacific Gas &amp; Electric">Pacific Gas &amp; Electric</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT8613_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT8613-90">[86]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-MasonJones2012-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-MasonJones2012_34-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Per this source:<br/> :<cite class="citation" id="CITEREFMason-Jones2012">Mason-Jones, David (2012), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=2Lkp8jmFsFEC&amp;pg=PT103" rel="nofollow"><i>Should Meat be on the Menu?</i></a>, Momentum, p. 103, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1743340605" title="Special:BookSources/1743340605">1743340605</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Should+Meat+be+on+the+Menu%3F&amp;rft.pages=103&amp;rft.pub=Momentum&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.isbn=1743340605&amp;rft.aulast=Mason-Jones&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D2Lkp8jmFsFEC%26pg%3DPT103&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAtmospheric+methane"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><br/> research in the Snowy Mountains region of Australia showed 8 tonnes of methane oxidized by methanotrophic bacteria per year on a 1,000 hectare farm. 200 cows on the same farm emitted 5.4 tonnes of methane per year. Hence, one cow emitted 27 kg of methane per year, while the bacteria oxidized 8 kg per hectare. The emissions of one cow were oxidized by 27/8 ≈ 3.4 hectare.</span>
</li>
